</td></tr></tbody></table></section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(11)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="Controversies">Controversies</span></h2><section class="mf-section-11 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-11">
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Criticism_of_Amazon" title="Criticism of Amazon">Criticism of Amazon</a></div>
<p>Since its founding, the company has attracted criticism and controversy for its actions, including: supplying law enforcement with facial recognition surveillance tools;<sup id="cite_ref-washingtonpost.com_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-washingtonpost.com-141">[141]</a></sup> forming cloud computing partnerships with the <a href="/wiki/CIA" class="mw-redirect" title="CIA">CIA</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-vanity-fair_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vanity-fair-142">[142]</a></sup> leading customers away from bookshops;<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> adversely impacting the environment;<sup id="cite_ref-Cavaillez_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cavaillez-144">[144]</a></sup> placing a low priority on warehouse conditions for workers; actively opposing unionization efforts;<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> remotely deleting content purchased by Amazon Kindle users; taking public subsidies; seeking to patent its <a href="/wiki/1-Click" title="1-Click">1-Click</a> technology; engaging in anti-competitive actions and <a href="/wiki/Price_discrimination" title="Price discrimination">price discrimination</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-:6_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:6-27">[27]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-baum_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-baum-28">[28]</a></sup> and reclassifying <a href="/wiki/LGBT" title="LGBT">LGBT</a> books as adult content.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> Criticism has also concerned various decisions over whether to censor or publish content such as the <a href="/wiki/WikiLeaks" title="WikiLeaks">WikiLeaks</a> website, works containing <a href="/wiki/Defamation" title="Defamation">libel</a> and material facilitating <a href="/wiki/Dog_fighting" title="Dog fighting">dogfight</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cockfight" title="Cockfight">cockfight</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Pedophilia" title="Pedophilia">pedophile</a> activities. In December 2011, Amazon faced a backlash from small businesses for running a one-day deal to promote its new Price Check app. Shoppers who used the app to check prices in a brick-and-mortar store were offered a 5% discount to purchase the same item from Amazon.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup> Companies like <a href="/wiki/Groupon" title="Groupon">Groupon</a>, <a href="/wiki/EBay" title="EBay">eBay</a> and Taap.it countered Amazon's promotion by offering $10 off from their products.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup> The company has also faced accusations of putting undue pressure on suppliers to maintain and extend its profitability. One effort to squeeze the most vulnerable book publishers was known within the company as the Gazelle Project, after Bezos suggested, according to <a href="/wiki/Brad_Stone_(journalist)" title="Brad Stone (journalist)">Brad Stone</a>, "that Amazon should approach these small publishers the way a cheetah would pursue a sickly gazelle."<sup id="cite_ref-New_Yorker_99-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-New_Yorker-99">[99]</a></sup> In July 2014, the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Trade_Commission" title="Federal Trade Commission">Federal Trade Commission</a> launched a lawsuit against the company alleging it was promoting in-app purchases to children, which were being transacted without parental consent.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup> In 2019, Amazon banned selling <a href="/wiki/Skin_whitening" title="Skin whitening">skin-lightening</a> and <a href="/wiki/Racism" title="Racism">racist</a> products that might effect the consumer health.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_impact">Environmental impact</span></h3>
<p>In 2018, Amazon emitted 44.4 million metric tons of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide"><span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br>2</span></span></span></a>.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup></p><p>In September 2019, Amazon workers organized a walk-out as part of the <a href="/wiki/Global_Climate_Strike_of_September_2019" class="mw-redirect" title="Global Climate Strike of September 2019">Global Climate Strike</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:4_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-154">[154]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:5_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-155">[155]</a></sup> An internal group called Amazon Employees for Climate Justice said over 1,800 employees in 25 cities and 14 countries committed to participating in the action to protest Amazon's environmental impact and inaction to <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">climate change</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:4_154-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-154">[154]</a></sup> This group of workers petitioned Jeff Bezos and Amazon with three specific demands: to stop donating to politicians and <a href="/wiki/Lobbying" title="Lobbying">lobbyists</a> that <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_denial" title="Climate change denial">deny climate change</a>, to stop working with <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuel</a> companies to accelerate oil and gas extraction, and to achieve zero <a href="/wiki/Carbon_emissions" class="mw-redirect" title="Carbon emissions">carbon emissions</a> by 2030.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:5_155-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-155">[155]</a></sup></p><p>Amazon has introduced the Shipment Zero program, however Shipment Zero has only committed to reducing 50% of its shipments to net zero by 2030. Also, even that 50% does not necessarily mean a decrease in emissions compared to current levels given Amazon's rate of growth in orders.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup></p><p>That said, Amazon's CEO has also signed the Climate Pledge, in which Amazon would meet the Paris climate agreement goals 10 year ahead of schedule, and would be carbon-neutral by 2040. Besides this pledge, it also ordered 100 000 electric delivery trucks from <a href="/wiki/Rivian" title="Rivian">Rivian</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-159">[159]</a></sup></p><p>Amazon funds both climate denial groups including the <a href="/wiki/Competitive_Enterprise_Institute" title="Competitive Enterprise Institute">Competitive Enterprise Institute</a> and politicians denying climate change including <a href="/wiki/Jim_Inhofe" title="Jim Inhofe">Jim Inhofe</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup></p><p>In November 2018, a community action group opposed the construction permit delivered to <a href="/wiki/Goodman_Group" title="Goodman Group">Goodman Group</a> for the construction of a 160,000 square metres (1,700,000 sq ft) logisitics platform Amazon will operate at <a href="/wiki/Lyon%E2%80%93Saint-Exup%C3%A9ry_Airport" title="Lyon–Saint-Exupéry Airport">Lyon–Saint-Exupéry Airport</a>. In February 2019, <a href="https://fr.wikipedia.org/wiki/%C3%89tienne_T%C3%AAte" class="extiw" title="fr:Étienne Tête">Étienne Tête</a> filed a request on behalf of a second regional community action group asking the administrative court to decide whether the platform served a sufficiently important public interest to justify its environmental impact. Construction has been suspended while these matters are decided.<sup id="cite_ref-Cavaillez_144-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cavaillez-144">[144]</a></sup></p><p>Amazon considered making an option for Prime customers to have packages delivered at the most efficient and environmentally-friendly time (allowing the company to combine shipments with the same destination) but decided against it out of fear customers might reduce purchases.<sup id="cite_ref-day_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-day-162">[162]</a></sup> Since 2019, the company has instead offered customer an "Amazon Day" option, where all orders are delivered on the same day, emphasizing customer convenience, and it occasionally offers Prime customers credits in return for selecting slower and less expensive shipping options.<sup id="cite_ref-day_162-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-day-162">[162]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Selling_counterfeit_items">Selling counterfeit items</span></h3>
<p>On October 16, 2016, <a href="/wiki/Apple_Inc." title="Apple Inc.">Apple</a> filed a trademark infringement case against Mobile Star LLC for selling counterfeit Apple products to Amazon. In the suit, Apple provided evidence that Amazon was selling these counterfeit Apple products and advertising them as genuine. Through purchasing, Apple found that it was able to identify counterfeit products with a success rate of 90%. Amazon was sourcing and selling items without properly determining if they are genuine. Mobile Star LLC settled with Apple for an undisclosed amount on April 27, 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Sales_and_use_taxes">Sales and use taxes</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Amazon_tax" title="Amazon tax">Amazon tax</a></div>
<p>Amazon's state sales tax collection policy has changed over the years since it did not collect any sales taxes in its early years. In the U.S., <a href="/wiki/Sales_taxes_in_the_United_States" title="Sales taxes in the United States">state and local sales taxes</a> are levied by state and local governments, not at the federal level. In most countries where Amazon operates, a sales tax or <a href="/wiki/Value_added_tax" class="mw-redirect" title="Value added tax">value added tax</a> is uniform throughout the country, and Amazon is obliged to collect it from all customers. Proponents of forcing Amazon.com to collect sales tax—at least in states where it maintains a physical presence—argue the corporation wields an anticompetitive advantage over storefront businesses forced to collect sales tax.<sup id="cite_ref-Bloomberg/BusinessWeek_164-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bloomberg/BusinessWeek-164">[164]</a></sup></p><p>Many U.S. states in the 21st century have passed <a href="/wiki/Online_shopping" title="Online shopping">online shopping</a> sales tax laws designed to compel Amazon.com and other <a href="/wiki/E-commerce" title="E-commerce">e-commerce</a> retailers to collect state and local sales taxes from its customers. Amazon.com originally collected sales tax only from five states as of 2011<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Amazon_(company)&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, but as of April 2017<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Amazon_(company)&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, Amazon collects sales taxes from customers in all 45 states that have a state sales tax and in <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington, D.C.</a><sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Income_taxes">Income taxes</span></h3>
<p>Amazon paid no <a href="/wiki/Income_tax_in_the_United_States" title="Income tax in the United States">federal income taxes</a> in the U.S. in 2017 and 2018, and actually received tax refunds worth millions of dollars, despite recording several billion dollars in profits each year.<sup id="cite_ref-fortune_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fortune-26">[26]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/CNN" title="CNN">CNN</a> reported that Amazon's tax bill was zero because they took advantage of provisions in years when they were losing money that allowed them to <a href="/wiki/Net_operating_loss#Loss_carry_back_and_carry_forward_mechanisms" title="Net operating loss">offset future taxes on profits</a>, as well as various other tax credits.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup> Amazon was criticized by political figures for not paying federal income taxes.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Comments_by_Donald_Trump_and_Bernie_Sanders">Comments by Donald Trump and Bernie Sanders</span></h3>
<p>In early 2018, President <a href="/wiki/Donald_Trump" title="Donald Trump">Donald Trump</a> repeatedly criticized Amazon's use of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Postal_Service" title="United States Postal Service">United States Postal Service</a> and its prices for the delivery of packages, stating, "I am right about Amazon costing the United States Post Office massive amounts of money for being their Delivery Boy," Trump tweeted. "Amazon should pay these costs (plus) and not have them bourne [sic] by the American Taxpayer."<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup> Amazon's shares fell by 6 percent as a result of Trump's comments. <a href="/wiki/Shepard_Smith" title="Shepard Smith">Shepard Smith</a> of <a href="/wiki/Fox_News" title="Fox News">Fox News</a> disputed Trump's claims and pointed to evidence that the USPS was offering below-market prices to all customers with no advantage to Amazon. However, analyst Tom Forte pointed to the fact that Amazon's payments to the USPS are not made public and that their contract has a reputation for being "a sweetheart deal".<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup></p><p>Throughout the summer of 2018, Vermont Senator <a href="/wiki/Bernie_Sanders" title="Bernie Sanders">Bernie Sanders</a> criticized Amazon's wages and working conditions in a series of <a href="/wiki/YouTube" title="YouTube">YouTube</a> videos and media appearances. He also pointed to the fact that Amazon had paid no federal income tax in the previous year.<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup> Sanders solicited stories from Amazon warehouse workers who felt exploited by the company.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-172">[172]</a></sup> One such story, by <a href="/wiki/James_Bloodworth_(journalist)" title="James Bloodworth (journalist)">James Bloodworth</a>, described the environment as akin to "a low-security prison" and stated that the company's culture used an Orwellian <a href="/wiki/Newspeak" title="Newspeak">newspeak</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup> These reports cited a finding by New Food Economy that one third of fulfilment center workers in Arizona were on the <a href="/wiki/Supplemental_Nutrition_Assistance_Program" title="Supplemental Nutrition Assistance Program">Supplemental Nutrition Assistance Program</a> (SNAP).<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup> Responses by Amazon included incentives for employees to tweet positive stories and a statement which called the salary figures used by Sanders "inaccurate and misleading". The statement also charged that it was inappropriate for him to refer to SNAP as "food stamps".<sup id="cite_ref-:2_172-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-172">[172]</a></sup> On September 5, 2018, Sanders along with <a href="/wiki/Ro_Khanna" title="Ro Khanna">Ro Khanna</a> introduced the Stop Bad Employers by Zeroing Out Subsidies (Stop <a href="/wiki/Jeff_Bezos" title="Jeff Bezos">BEZOS</a>) Act aimed at Amazon and other alleged beneficiaries of <a href="/wiki/Corporate_welfare" title="Corporate welfare">corporate welfare</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Walmart" title="Walmart">Walmart</a>, <a href="/wiki/McDonald%27s" title="McDonald's">McDonald's</a> and <a href="/wiki/Uber" title="Uber">Uber</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup> Among the bill's supporters were <a href="/wiki/Tucker_Carlson" title="Tucker Carlson">Tucker Carlson</a> of <a href="/wiki/Fox_News" title="Fox News">Fox News</a> and <a href="/wiki/Matt_Taibbi" title="Matt Taibbi">Matt Taibbi</a> who criticized himself and other journalists for not covering Amazon's contribution to wealth inequality earlier.<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup></p><p>On October 2, Amazon announced that its minimum wage for all American employees would be raised to $15 per hour. Sanders congratulated the company for making this decision.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Working_conditions">Working conditions</span></h3>
<p>Former employees, current employees, the media, and politicians have criticized Amazon for poor working conditions at the company.<sup id="cite_ref-linkedin_179-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-linkedin-179">[179]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup> In 2011, it was publicized that workers had to carry out tasks in 100 °F (38 °C) heat  at the <a href="/wiki/Breinigsville,_Pennsylvania" title="Breinigsville, Pennsylvania">Breinigsville, Pennsylvania</a> warehouse. As a result of these inhumane conditions, employees became extremely uncomfortable and suffered from dehydration and <a href="/wiki/Collapse_(medical)" title="Collapse (medical)">collapse</a>. Loading-bay doors were not opened to allow in fresh air because of concerns over theft.<sup id="cite_ref-AllentownMorningCall1_182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AllentownMorningCall1-182">[182]</a></sup> Amazon's initial response was to pay for an ambulance to sit outside on call to cart away overheated employees.<sup id="cite_ref-AllentownMorningCall1_182-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AllentownMorningCall1-182">[182]</a></sup> The company eventually installed air conditioning at the warehouse.<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">[183]</a></sup></p><p>Some workers, "pickers", who travel the building with a trolley and a handheld scanner "picking" customer orders can walk up to 15 miles during their workday and if they fall behind on their targets, they can be reprimanded. The handheld scanners give real-time information to the employee on how quickly or slowly they are working; the scanners also serve to allow Team Leads and Area Managers to track the specific locations of employees and how much "idle time" they gain when not working.<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">[184]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup></p><p>In a German television report broadcast in February 2013, journalists Diana Löbl and Peter Onneken conducted a covert investigation at the distribution center of Amazon in the town of <a href="/wiki/Bad_Hersfeld" title="Bad Hersfeld">Bad Hersfeld</a> in the German state of <a href="/wiki/Hessen" class="mw-redirect" title="Hessen">Hessen</a>. The report highlights the behavior of some of the security guards, themselves being employed by a third party company, who apparently either had a <a href="/wiki/Neo-Nazism" title="Neo-Nazism">neo-Nazi</a> background or deliberately dressed in neo-Nazi apparel and who were intimidating foreign and temporary female workers at its distribution centers. The third party security company involved was delisted by Amazon as a business contact shortly after that report.<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">[187]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">[188]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">[189]</a></sup></p><p>In March 2015, it was reported in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Verge" title="The Verge">The Verge</a></i> that Amazon will be removing <a href="/wiki/Non-compete_clause" title="Non-compete clause">non-compete clauses</a> of 18 months in length from its US employment contracts for hourly-paid workers, after criticism that it was acting unreasonably in preventing such employees from finding other work. Even short-term temporary workers have to sign contracts that prohibit them from working at any company where they would "directly or indirectly" support any good or service that competes with those they helped support at Amazon, for 18 months after leaving Amazon, even if they are fired or made redundant.<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">[190]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup></p><p>A 2015 front-page article in <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i> profiled several former Amazon employees<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">[192]</a></sup> who together described a "bruising" workplace culture in which workers with illness or other personal crises were pushed out or unfairly evaluated.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-20">[20]</a></sup> Bezos responded by writing a Sunday memo to employees,<sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">[193]</a></sup> in which he disputed the <i>Times</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s account of "shockingly callous management practices" that he said would never be tolerated at the company.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_20-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-20">[20]</a></sup></p><p>In an effort to boost employee morale, on November 2, 2015, Amazon announced that it would be extending six weeks of paid leave for new mothers and fathers. This change includes birth parents and adoptive parents and can be applied in conjunction with existing maternity leave and medical leave for new mothers.<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">[194]</a></sup></p><p>In mid-2018, investigations by journalists and media outlets such as <i><a href="/wiki/The_Guardian" title="The Guardian">The Guardian</a></i> reported poor working conditions at Amazon's fulfillment centers.<sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">[195]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">[196]</a></sup> Later in 2018, another article exposed poor working conditions for Amazon's delivery drivers.<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">[197]</a></sup></p><p>In response to criticism that Amazon does not pay its workers a livable wage, Jeff Bezos announced beginning November 1, 2018, all US and UK Amazon employees will earn a $15 an hour minimum wage.<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">[198]</a></sup> Amazon will also lobby to make $15 an hour the federal <a href="/wiki/Minimum_wage" title="Minimum wage">minimum wage</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">[199]</a></sup> At the same time, Amazon also eliminated stock awards and bonuses for hourly employees.<sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">[200]</a></sup></p><p>On <a href="/wiki/Black_Friday_(shopping)" title="Black Friday (shopping)">Black Friday</a> 2018, Amazon warehouse workers in several European countries, including Italy, Germany, Spain and the United Kingdom, went on strike to protest inhumane working conditions and low pay.<sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">[201]</a></sup></p><p><i><a href="/wiki/The_Daily_Beast" title="The Daily Beast">The Daily Beast</a></i> reported in March 2019 that emergency services responded to 189 calls from 46 Amazon warehouses in 17 states between the years 2013 and 2018, all relating to suicidal employees. The workers attributed their mental breakdowns to employer-imposed social isolation, aggressive surveillance, and the hurried and dangerous working conditions at these fulfillment centers. One former employee told <i>The Daily Beast</i> "It's this isolating colony of hell where people having breakdowns is a regular occurrence."<sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">[202]</a></sup></p><p>On July 15, 2019, during the onset of Amazon's "Prime Day" sale event, Amazon employees working in the United States and Germany went on strike in protest of unfair wages and poor working conditions.<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">[203]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">[204]</a></sup></p><p>In March 2020, during the coronavirus outbreak when the government instructed companies to restrict social contact, Amazon's UK staff was forced to work overtime to meet the demand spiked by the disease. A <a href="/wiki/GMB_(trade_union)" title="GMB (trade union)">GMB (trade union)</a> spokesperson said the company had put "profit before safety".<sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">[205]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Conflict_of_interest_with_the_CIA_and_DOD">Conflict of interest with the CIA and DOD</span></h3>
<p>In 2013, Amazon secured a <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$600 million</span> contract with the <a href="/wiki/CIA" class="mw-redirect" title="CIA">CIA</a>, which poses a potential <a href="/wiki/Conflict_of_interest" title="Conflict of interest">conflict of interest</a> involving the Bezos-owned <i><a href="/wiki/The_Washington_Post" title="The Washington Post">The Washington Post</a></i> and his newspaper's coverage of the CIA.<sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206">[206]</a></sup> Kate Martin, director of the Center for National Security Studies, said, "It's a serious potential conflict of interest for a major newspaper like <i>The Washington Post</i> to have a contractual relationship with the government and the most secret part of the government."<sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">[207]</a></sup> This was later followed by a <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$10 billion</span> contract with the Department of Defense.<sup id="cite_ref-vanity-fair_142-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vanity-fair-142">[142]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Seattle_head_tax_and_houselessness_services">Seattle head tax and houselessness services</span></h3>
<p>In May 2018, Amazon threatened the <a href="/wiki/Seattle_City_Council" title="Seattle City Council">Seattle City Council</a> over an <a href="/wiki/Seattle_head_tax" title="Seattle head tax">employee head tax proposal</a> that would have funded houselessness services and low-income housing. The tax would have cost Amazon about $800 per employee, or 0.7% of their average salary.<sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">[208]</a></sup> In retaliation, Amazon paused construction on a new building, threatened to limit further investment in the city, and funded a repeal campaign. Although originally passed, the measure was soon repealed after an expensive repeal campaign spearheaded by Amazon.<sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">[209]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Nashville_Operations_Center_of_Excellence">Nashville Operations Center of Excellence</span></h3>
<p>The incentives given by the <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_Council_of_Nashville_and_Davidson_County" title="Metropolitan Council of Nashville and Davidson County">Metropolitan Council of Nashville and Davidson County</a> to Amazon for their new Operations Center of Excellence in <a href="/w/index.php?title=Nashville_Yards&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Nashville Yards (page does not exist)">Nashville Yards</a>, a site owned by developer <a href="/wiki/Southwest_Value_Partners" title="Southwest Value Partners">Southwest Value Partners</a>, have been controversial, including the decision by the Tennessee Department of Economic and Community Development to keep the full extent of the agreement secret.<sup id="cite_ref-tenneseanwantstokeepits_210-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tenneseanwantstokeepits-210">[210]</a></sup> The incentives include "$102 million in combined grants and tax credits for a scaled-down Amazon office building" as well as "a $65 million cash grant for capital expenditures" in exchange for the creation of 5,000 jobs over seven years.<sup id="cite_ref-tenneseanwantstokeepits_210-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tenneseanwantstokeepits-210">[210]</a></sup></p><p>The Tennessee Coalition for Open Government called for more transparency.<sup id="cite_ref-tenneseanwantstokeepits_210-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tenneseanwantstokeepits-210">[210]</a></sup> Another local organization known as the People's Alliance for Transit, Housing, and Employment (PATHE) suggested no public money should be given to Amazon; instead, it should be spent on building more public housing for the working poor and the homeless and investing in more public transportation for Nashvillians.<sup id="cite_ref-tntribunewillamazonbe_211-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tntribunewillamazonbe-211">[211]</a></sup> Others suggested incentives to big corporations do not improve the local economy.<sup id="cite_ref-tennesseandidtennesseetaxpayers_212-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tennesseandidtennesseetaxpayers-212">[212]</a></sup></p><p>In November 2018, the proposal to give Amazon $15 million in incentives was criticized by the Nashville Firefighters Union and the Nashville chapter of the <a href="/wiki/Fraternal_Order_of_Police" title="Fraternal Order of Police">Fraternal Order of Police</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-newschannel5feelleftbehind_213-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-newschannel5feelleftbehind-213">[213]</a></sup> who called it "<a href="/wiki/Corporate_welfare" title="Corporate welfare">corporate welfare</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-apnewsnashvillepoliceunion_214-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-apnewsnashvillepoliceunion-214">[214]</a></sup> In February 2019, another $15.2 million in infrastructure was approved by the council, although it was voted down by three council members, including Councilwoman Angie Henderson who dismissed it as "<a href="/wiki/Cronyism" title="Cronyism">cronyism</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-tennesseancouncilapproves_215-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tennesseancouncilapproves-215">[215]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Facial_recognition_technology_and_law_enforcement">Facial recognition technology and law enforcement</span></h3>
<p>While Amazon has publicly opposed secret government surveillance, as revealed by Freedom of Information Act requests it has supplied <a href="/wiki/Facial_recognition_system" title="Facial recognition system">facial recognition</a> support to law enforcement in the form of the <a href="/wiki/Amazon_Rekognition" title="Amazon Rekognition">Rekognition</a> technology and consulting services. Initial testing included the city of <a href="/wiki/Orlando,_Florida" title="Orlando, Florida">Orlando, Florida</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Washington_County,_Oregon" title="Washington County, Oregon">Washington County, Oregon</a>. Amazon offered to connect Washington County with other Amazon government customers interested in Rekognition and a body camera manufacturer. These ventures are opposed by a coalition of civil rights groups with concern that they could lead to an expansion of surveillance and be prone to abuse. Specifically, it could automate the identification and tracking of anyone, particularly in the context of potential police body camera integration.<sup id="cite_ref-washingtonpost.com_141-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-washingtonpost.com-141">[141]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216">[216]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217">[217]</a></sup> Because of the backlash, the city of <a href="/wiki/Orlando,_Florida" title="Orlando, Florida">Orlando</a> publicly stated it will no longer use the technology, but may revisit this decision at a later date.<sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218">[218]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Access_to_NHS_data">Access to NHS data</span></h3>
<p>The UK government awarded Amazon a contract that gives the company free access to healthcare information collected by the UK's National Health Service<sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219">[219]</a></sup>. This will let people have their medical questions answered by Amazon's Alexa. The material, which excludes patient data, could also allow the company to make, advertise and sell its own products. The contract allows Amazon access to information on symptoms, causes and definitions of conditions, and "all related copyrightable content and data and other materials". Amazon can then create "new products, applications, cloud-based services and/or distributed software", which the NHS will not benefit from financially. The company can also share the information with third parties. The government said that allowing Alexa devices to offer expert health advice to users will reduce pressure on doctors and pharmacists<sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220">[220]</a></sup>.
</p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Collection_of_data_and_surveillance">Collection of data and surveillance</span></h3>
<p>On February 17, 2020, a Panorama documentary highlighted the amount of data collected by the company and the move into surveillance causing concerns of politicians and regulators in the US and Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221">[221]</a></sup> <sup id="cite_ref-222" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-222">[222]</a></sup></p>
</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(12)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="Response_to_Covid-19_pandemic">Response to Covid-19 pandemic</span></h2><section class="mf-section-12 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-12">
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Additional_hiring_as_a_result_of_pandemic">Additional hiring as a result of pandemic</span></h3>
<p>In response to the COVID-19 pandemic, Amazon introduced temporary restrictions on the sale of non-essential goods. In March 2020, it hired some 100,000 more staff in the US to help deal with essential items such as food and medical equipment. It also reported that it was so busy that it was unable to bring on board new customers and therefore had to have a waiting list. In April, the firm announced that it was going to hire up to 75,000 workers to help deal with inreased demand.<sup id="cite_ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223">[223]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Employee_protests_during_Covid-19">Employee protests during Covid-19</span></h3>
<p>During the outbreak of the <a href="/wiki/Coronavirus_pandemic" class="mw-redirect" title="Coronavirus pandemic">Coronavirus pandemic</a> (COVID-19) there have been protests by the Amazon workers at warehouses in the US, France, and Italy. The BBC reported that there were confirmed  coronavirus cases in more than 50 locations.<sup id="cite_ref-224" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-224">[224]</a></sup> The reason for the protests is the company policy to "run normal shifts" despite many positive cases of the virus.<sup id="cite_ref-FTMarch19_225-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FTMarch19-225">[225]</a></sup> According to the <a href="/wiki/UNI_Global_Union" title="UNI Global Union">UNI Global Union</a>, "Amazon cannot act like this is business as usual. We are facing a deadly virus that has already taken the lives of thousands of people and paralyzed the world's economy. If distribution centers are not safe for workers right now, they should be closed immediately."<sup id="cite_ref-FTMarch19_225-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FTMarch19-225">[225]</a></sup> In Spain, the company has faced legal complaints over its policies.<sup id="cite_ref-BBCMarch31_226-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBCMarch31-226">[226]</a></sup> Despite workers at 19 warehouses in the US having tested positive for COVID-19, Amazon did not shut down warehouses, only doing so when forced by the government or because of protests. A group of US Senators wrote an open letter to Bezos in March 2020, expressing concerns about worker safety.<sup id="cite_ref-TheVergeMarch31_227-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TheVergeMarch31-227">[227]</a></sup></p><p>An Amazon warehouse protest on March 30, 2020 in <a href="/wiki/Staten_Island" title="Staten Island">Staten Island</a> led to its organizer, Christian Smalls, being fired. Amazon defended the decision by saying that Smalls was supposed to be in self-isolation at the time and leading the protest put its other workers at risk.<sup id="cite_ref-BBCMarch31_226-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBCMarch31-226">[226]</a></sup> Smalls has called this response "ridiculous".<sup id="cite_ref-CommonDreamsMarch31_228-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CommonDreamsMarch31-228">[228]</a></sup> The New York state attorney general, <a href="/wiki/Letitia_James" title="Letitia James">Letitia James</a>, is considering legal retaliation to the firing which she called "immoral and inhumane."<sup id="cite_ref-BBCMarch31_226-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBCMarch31-226">[226]</a></sup> She also asked the <a href="/wiki/National_Labor_Relations_Board" title="National Labor Relations Board">National Labor Relations Board</a> to investigate Smalls' firing. Smalls himself accuses the company of retaliating against him for organizing a protest.<sup id="cite_ref-CommonDreamsMarch31_228-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CommonDreamsMarch31-228">[228]</a></sup> At the Staten Island warehouse, one case of COVID-19 has been confirmed by Amazon; workers believe there are more, and say that the company has not cleaned the building, given them suitable protection, or informed them of potential cases.<sup id="cite_ref-TheVergeMarch31_227-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TheVergeMarch31-227">[227]</a></sup> Smalls added specifically that there are many workers there in risk categories, and the protest only demanded that the building be sanitized and the employees continue to be paid during that process.<sup id="cite_ref-CommonDreamsMarch31_228-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CommonDreamsMarch31-228">[228]</a></sup> Derrick Palmer, another worker at the Staten Island facility, told <i><a href="/wiki/The_Verge" title="The Verge">The Verge</a></i> that Amazon quickly communicates through text and email when they need the staff to complete mandatory overtime, but have not been using this to tell people when a colleague has contracted the disease, instead waiting days and sending managers to speak to employees in person.<sup id="cite_ref-TheVergeMarch31_227-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TheVergeMarch31-227">[227]</a></sup> Amazon claim that the Staten Island protest only attracted 15 of the facility's 5,000 workers,<sup id="cite_ref-229" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-229">[229]</a></sup> while other sources describe much larger crowds.<sup id="cite_ref-TheVergeMarch31_227-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TheVergeMarch31-227">[227]</a></sup></p><p>On April 14, 2020, two Amazon employees were fired for "repeatedly violating internal policies", after they had circulated a petition about health risks for warehouse workers internally <sup id="cite_ref-230" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-230">[230]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Closure_in_France">Closure in France</span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Solidaires_Unitaires_D%C3%A9mocratiques" title="Solidaires Unitaires Démocratiques">SUD</a> (trade unions) brought a court case against Amazon for unsafe working conditions. This resulted in a French district court (<a href="/wiki/Nanterre" title="Nanterre">Nanterre</a>) ruling on April 15, 2020 ordering the company to limit its deliveries to food and essential medical or hygienic products or pay a €1 million  per day fine.  Amazon said it would appeal, and on the following day shut its six French warehouses until at least April 21, 2020 for evaluation of the situation.<sup id="cite_ref-231" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-231">[231]</a></sup></p>
</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(13)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="Lobbying">Lobbying</span></h2><section class="mf-section-13 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-13">
<p>Amazon lobbies the United States federal government and state governments on multiple issues such as the enforcement of sales taxes on online sales, transportation safety, privacy and <a href="/wiki/Information_privacy" title="Information privacy">data protection</a> and intellectual property. According to regulatory filings, Amazon.com focuses its lobbying on the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">United States Congress</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Communications_Commission" title="Federal Communications Commission">Federal Communications Commission</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Reserve_System" class="mw-redirect" title="Federal Reserve System">Federal Reserve</a>. Amazon.com spent roughly $3.5 million, $5 million and $9.5 million on lobbying, in 2013, 2014 and 2015, respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-232" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-232">[232]</a></sup></p><p>Amazon.com was a corporate member of the <a href="/wiki/American_Legislative_Exchange_Council" title="American Legislative Exchange Council">American Legislative Exchange Council</a> (ALEC) until it dropped membership following protests at its shareholders' meeting on May 24, 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-233" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-233">[233]</a></sup></p><p>In 2014, Amazon expanded its lobbying practices as it prepared to lobby the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Aviation_Administration" title="Federal Aviation Administration">Federal Aviation Administration</a> to approve its drone delivery program, hiring the <a href="/wiki/Akin_Gump_Strauss_Hauer_%26_Feld" title="Akin Gump Strauss Hauer &amp; Feld">Akin Gump Strauss Hauer &amp; Feld</a> lobbying firm in June.<sup id="cite_ref-234" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-234">[234]</a></sup> Amazon and its lobbyists have visited with Federal Aviation Administration officials and aviation committees in Washington, D.C. to explain its plans to deliver packages.<sup id="cite_ref-235" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-235">[235]</a></sup></p><p>In 2019 it spent $16.8m and had a team of 104 lobbyists, up from $14.4m and 103 lobbyists in 2018.<sup id="cite_ref-236" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-236">[236]</a></sup></p>
</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(14)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span></h2><section class="mf-section-14 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-14">
